<p>The <b>Eurasian eagle-owl</b> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) is a species of <a href="/wiki/Eagle-owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eagle-owl">eagle-owl</a> that resides in much of <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Eurasia</a>. It is also called the <b>European eagle-owl</b> and in Europe, it is occasionally abbreviated to just the <b>eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the largest species of <a href="/wiki/Owl" title="Owl">owl</a>, and females can grow to a total length of 75&#160;cm (30&#160;in), with a wingspan of 188&#160;cm (6&#160;ft 2&#160;in), males being slightly smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> This bird has distinctive ear tufts, with upper parts that are mottled with darker blackish colouring and tawny. The wings and tail are barred. The underparts are a variably hued buff, streaked with darker colour. The facial disc is not very visible and the orange eyes are distinctive.
</p><p>The Eurasian eagle-owl is one of the largest living species of owl as well as one of the most widely distributed.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl is found in many habitats but is mostly a bird of mountain regions, coniferous forests, steppes and other relatively remote places, and have occasionally been found near farmland and in <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">park-like</a> settings within European cities; in 2020, a brood of three chicks were raised by their mother on a large, well-foliaged planter on an apartment window in the city centre of <a href="/wiki/Geel" title="Geel">Geel, Belgium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OwlsWatchTV_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OwlsWatchTV-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It is a mostly nocturnal predator, hunting for a range of different prey species, predominantly small mammals but also birds of varying sizes, reptiles, amphibians, fish, large insects and other assorted invertebrates. It typically breeds on cliff ledges, in gullies, among rocks or in other concealed locations. The nest is a scrape in which averages of two eggs are laid at intervals. These hatch at different times. The female incubates the eggs and broods the young, and the male provides food for her and, when they hatch, for the nestlings as well. Continuing parental care for the young is provided by both adults for about five months.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> There are at least 12 <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of the Eurasian eagle-owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With a total range in Europe and Asia of about 32 million square kilometres (12 million square miles) and a total population estimated to be between 250 thousand and 2.5 million, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a> lists the bird's conservation status as being of "<a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  The vast majority of eagle-owls live in mainland Europe, Russia and Central Asia, and an estimated number of between 12 and 40 pairs are thought to reside in the United Kingdom as of 2016, a number which may be on the rise.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Tame eagle-owls have occasionally been used in pest control because of their size to <a href="/wiki/Nuisance_wildlife_management" title="Nuisance wildlife management">deter</a> large birds such as <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a> from nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg/220px-Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg/330px-Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg/440px-Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_RWD_at_CRC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Note the orange eyes and vertical stripes on the chest</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl is a very large bird, smaller than the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>) but larger than the <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> (<i>Bubo scandiacus</i>), despite some overlap in size with both species. It is sometimes referred to as the world's largest owl,<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Blakiston%27s_fish_owl" title="Blakiston&#39;s fish owl">Blakiston's fish owl</a> (<i>B. blakistoni</i>) is slightly heavier on average and the much lighter weight <a href="/wiki/Great_grey_owl" title="Great grey owl">great grey owl</a> (<i>Strix nebulosa</i>) is slightly longer on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Slaght_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slaght-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Heimo Mikkola reported the largest specimens of eagle-owl as having the same upper body mass, 4.6&#160;kg (10&#160;lb), as the largest Blakiston’s fish owl and attained a length of around 3&#160;cm (1.2&#160;in) longer.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In terms of average weight and wing size, the Blakiston’s is the slightly larger species seemingly, even averaging a bit larger in these aspects than the biggest eagle-owl races from Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Slaght_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Slaght-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Also, although 9&#160;cm (3.5&#160;in) shorter than the largest of the latter species, the Eurasian eagle-owl can weigh well more than twice as much as the largest great grey owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl typically has a wingspan of 131–188&#160;cm (4&#160;ft 4&#160;in–6&#160;ft 2&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> with the largest specimens possibly attaining 200&#160;cm (6&#160;ft 7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The total length of the species can vary from 56 to 75&#160;cm (22 to 30&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Females can weigh from 1.75 to 4.6&#160;kg (3.9 to 10.1&#160;lb) and males can weigh from 1.22 to 3.2&#160;kg (2.7 to 7.1&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In comparison, the <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), the world's most widely distributed owl species, weighs about 500&#160;g (1.1&#160;lb) and the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> (<i>Bubo virginianus</i>), which fills the eagle-owl's <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> in North America, weighs around 1.4&#160;kg (3.1&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg/220px-Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg/330px-Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg/440px-Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1747" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_eagle_owl_and_handler_arp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian eagle-owl in captivity</div></div></div>
<p>Besides the female being larger, there is little external sexual dimorphism in the Eurasian eagle-owl, although the ear tufts of males reportedly tend to be more upright than those of females.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> When an eagle-owl is seen on its own in the field, it is generally not possible to distinguish the individual’s sex. Gender determination by size is possible via in hand measurements.<sup id="cite_ref-delMarDelgado_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delMarDelgado-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Reportedly, in some populations the female may be slightly darker on average than the male.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The plumage coloration across at least 13 accepted subspecies can be highly variable. The upper parts may be brown-black to tawny-buff to pale creamy gray, typically showing dense freckling on the forehead and crown, stripes on the nape, sides and back of the neck, and dark splotches on the pale ground colour of the back, mantle and scapulars. A narrow buff band, freckled with brown or buff, often runs up from the base of the bill, above the inner part of the eye and along the inner edge of the black-brown ear tufts. The rump and upper tail-coverts are delicately patterned with dark vermiculations and fine wavy barring, the extent of which varies with subspecies. The underwing coverts and undertail coverts are similar but tend to be more strongly barred in brownish-black.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Primaries" title="Flight feather">primaries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Secondaries" title="Flight feather">secondaries</a> are brown with broad dark brown bars and dark brown tips, and grey or buff irregular lines. A complete <a href="/wiki/Moulting#In_birds" title="Moulting">moult</a> takes place each year between July and December.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The facial disc is tawny-buff, speckled with black-brown, so densely on the outer edge of the disc as to form a "frame" around the face. The chin and throat are white with a brownish central streak. The feathers of the upper breast generally have brownish-black centres and reddish-brown edges except for the central ones which have white edges. The chin and throat may appear white continuing down the center of the upper breast. The lower breast and belly feathers are creamy-brown to tawny buff to off-white with a variable amount of fine dark wavy barring, on a tawny-buff ground colour. The legs and feet (which are feathered almost to the talons) are likewise marked on a buff ground colour but more faintly. The tail is tawny-buff, mottled dark grey-brown with about six black-brown bars. The bill and feet are black. The iris is most often orange but is fairly variable. In some European birds, the iris is a bright reddish, blood-orange colour but then in subspecies found in arid, desert-like habitats, the iris can range into an orange-yellow colour (most closely related species generally have yellowish irises, excluding the <a href="/wiki/Indian_eagle-owl" title="Indian eagle-owl">Indian eagle-owl</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_measurements_and_physiology">Standard measurements and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Standard measurements and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre,_Surrey,_England-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg/220px-Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg/330px-Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg/440px-Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre%2C_Surrey%2C_England-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="667" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_-British_Wildlife_Centre,_Surrey,_England-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The wings have a wide spread</div></div></div>
<p>Among standard measurements for the Eurasian eagle-owl, the <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> measures 378 to 518&#160;mm (14.9 to 20.4&#160;in), the tail measures 229–310&#160;mm (9.0–12.2&#160;in) long, the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> measures 64.5–112&#160;mm (2.54–4.41&#160;in) and the total length of the <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">bill</a> is 38.9–59&#160;mm (1.53–2.32&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The wings are reportedly the smallest in proportion to the body weight of any European owl, when measured by the grams per square cm of wing size, was found to be 0.72. Thus they have quite high <a href="/wiki/Wing_loading" title="Wing loading">wing loading</a>. The great horned owl has even smaller wings (0.8 grams per square cm) relative to its body size. The golden eagle has slightly lower wing loading proportionately (0.65 grams per square cm), so the aerial abilities of the two species (beyond the eagle’s spectacular ability to stoop) may not be as disparate as expected. Some other owls, such as barn owls, <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>) and even the related snowy owls have lower wing loading relative to their size and so are presumably able to fly faster, with more agility and for more extended periods than the Eurasian eagle-owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> In the relatively small race <i>B. b. hispanus</i>, the middle claw, the largest talon, (as opposed to rear hallux-claw which is the largest in accipitrids) was found to measure from 21.6 to 40.1&#160;mm (0.85 to 1.58&#160;in) in length.<sup id="cite_ref-delMarDelgado_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delMarDelgado-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> A 3.82&#160;kg (8.4&#160;lb) female examined in Britain (origins unspecified) had a middle claw measuring 57.9&#160;mm (2.28&#160;in), on par in length with a large female golden eagle hallux-claw.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Generally, owls do not have talons as proportionately large as those of accipitrids but have stronger, more robust feet relative to their size. Accipitrids use their talons to inflict organ damage and blood loss, whereas typical owls use their feet to constrict their prey to death, the talons serving only to hold the prey in place or provide incidental damage. The talons of the Eurasian eagle-owl are very large and not often exceeded in size by diurnal raptors. Unlike the great horned owls, the overall foot size and strength of the Eurasian eagle-owl is not known to have been tested, but the considerably smaller horned owl has one of the strongest grips ever measured in a bird.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The feathers of the ear tufts in Spanish birds (when not damaged) were found to measure from 63.3 to 86.6&#160;mm (2.49 to 3.41&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-delMarDelgado_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-delMarDelgado-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The ear openings (covered in feathers as in all birds) are relatively uncomplicated but are also large, being larger on the right than on the left as in most owls, and proportionately larger than those of the great horned owl. In the female, the ear opening averages 31.7&#160;mm (1.25&#160;in) on the right and 27.4&#160;mm (1.08&#160;in) on the left and, in males, averages 26.8&#160;mm (1.06&#160;in) on the right and 24.4&#160;mm (0.96&#160;in) on the left.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The depth of the facial disc and the size and complexity of the ear opening are directly correlated to the importance of sound in an owl’s hunting behaviour. Examples of owls with more complicated ear structures and deeper facial disc are barn owls, <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owls</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Boreal_owl" title="Boreal owl">boreal owls</a> (<i>Aegolius funereus</i>). Given the uncomplicated structure of their ear openings and relatively shallow, undefined facial disc, hunting by ear is secondary to hunting by sight in the eagle-owl, this seems to be true for <i>Bubo</i> in general. It is likely that more sound-based hunters such as the aforementioned species focus their hunting activity in more complete darkness. Also owls with white throat patches such as the Eurasian eagle-owl are more likely to be active in low light conditions in the hours before and after sunrise and sunset rather than the darkest times in the middle of the night. The boreal and barn owls, to extend these examples, lack obvious visual cues such as white throat patches (puffed up in displaying eagle-owls), again indicative of primary activity being in darker periods.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Distinguishing_from_other_species">Distinguishing from other species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Distinguishing from other species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The great size, bulky, barrel-shaped build, erect ear tufts and orange eyes render this as a distinctive species. Other than general morphology, the above features differ markedly from those of two of the next largest subarctic owl species in Europe and western Asia, which are the great grey owl and the greyish to chocolate-brown <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>), both of which have no ear tufts and have a distinctly rounded head, rather than the blocky shape of the eagle-owl’s head.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> is obviously distinctive from most eagle-owls, but during winter the palest Eurasian eagle-owl race (<i>B. b. sibiricus</i>) can appear off-white. Nevertheless, the latter is still distinctively an ear-tufted Eurasian eagle-owl and lacks the pure white background colour and variable black spotting of the slightly smaller species (which has relatively tiny, vestigial ear tufts that have only been observed to have flared on rare occasions).<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The long-eared owl has a somewhat similar plumage to the eagle-owl but is considerably smaller (an average female eagle-owl may be twice as long and ten times heavier than an average long-eared owl).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/220px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/330px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg/440px-Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2818" data-file-height="3600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Eagle_Owl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unique camouflage pattern</div></div></div>
<p>Long-eared owls in <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> have vertical striping like that of the Eurasian eagle-owl, while long-eared owls in North America show a more horizontal striping like that of great horned owls. Whether these are examples of mimicry either way is unclear but it is known that both <i>Bubo</i> owls are serious predators of long-eared owls. The same discrepancy in underside streaking has also been noted in the Eurasian and American representations of the grey owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> A few other related species overlap minimally in range in Asia, mainly in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> and the southern reaches of the Eurasian eagle-owl’s range. Three <a href="/wiki/Fish_owl" title="Fish owl">fish owls</a> appear to overlap in range, the <a href="/wiki/Brown_fish_owl" title="Brown fish owl">brown</a> (<i>Ketupa zeylonensis</i>) in at least northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, probably <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a> and discontinuously in southern Turkey, the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_fish_owl" title="Tawny fish owl">tawny</a> (<i>K. flavipes</i>) through much of eastern China and Blakiston's fish owl in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>, northeastern China and <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>. Fish owls are distinctively different looking, possessing more scraggy ear tufts that hang to the side rather than sit erect on top of the head, generally have more uniform, brownish plumages without the contrasting darker streaking of an eagle-owl. The brown fish owl has no feathering on the tarsus or feet and the tawny has feathering only on the upper portion of the tarsi but the Blakiston’s is nearly as extensively feathered on the tarsi and feet as the eagle-owl. Tawny and brown fish owls are both slightly smaller than co-occurring Eurasian eagle-owls and Blakiston’s fish owls are similar or slightly larger than co-occurring large northern eagle-owls. Fish owls, being tied to the edges of freshwater where they hunt mainly fish and crabs, also have slightly differing, and more narrow, habitat preferences.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the lower <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> of northern Pakistan and Jammu and Kashmir, along with the brown fish owl, the Eurasian eagle-owl at the limit of its distribution may co-exist with at least two to three other eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> One of these, the <a href="/wiki/Dusky_eagle-owl" title="Dusky eagle-owl">dusky eagle-owl</a> (<i>B. coromandus</i>) is smaller, with more uniform tan-brownish plumage, untidy uniform light streaking rather than the Eurasian’s dark streaking below and an even less well-defined facial disc. The dusky is usually found in slightly more enclosed woodland areas than Eurasian eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Another is possibly the <a href="/wiki/Spot-bellied_eagle-owl" title="Spot-bellied eagle-owl">spot-bellied eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo nipalensis</i>), which is strikingly different looking, with stark brown plumage, rather than the warm hues typical of the Eurasian, bold spotting on a whitish background on the belly, and somewhat askew ear tufts that are bold white with light brown crossbars on the front. It is possible that both species occur in some parts of the Himalayan foothills but they are not currently verified to occur in the same area, in part because of the spot-bellied’s preference for dense, primary forest.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Most similar, with basically the same habitat preferences and the only one verified to co-occur with the Eurasian eagle-owls of the race <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> in Kashmir is the <a href="/wiki/Indian_eagle-owl" title="Indian eagle-owl">Indian eagle-owl</a> (<i>B. bengalensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The Indian species is smaller with a bolder blackish facial disc border, more rounded and relatively smaller wings and partially unfeathered toes. Far to the west, the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh_eagle-owl" title="Pharaoh eagle-owl">Pharaoh eagle-owl</a> (<i>B. ascalaphus</i>) also seemingly overlaps in range with the Eurasian, at least in the country of <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>. Although also relatively similar to the Eurasian eagle-owl, the Pharaoh eagle-owl is distinguished by its smaller size, paler, more washed-out plumage and the diminished size of its ear-tufts.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moulting">Moulting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Moulting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owls’ <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> are lightweight and robust but nevertheless need to be replaced periodically as they become worn. In the Eurasian eagle-owl, this happens in stages and the first moult starts the year after hatching with some body feathers and wing <a href="/wiki/Covert_feather" title="Covert feather">coverts</a> being replaced. The next year the three central <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Secondaries" title="Flight feather">secondaries</a> on each wing and three middle tail feathers are shed and regrow, and the following year two or three <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Primaries" title="Flight feather">primaries</a> and their coverts are lost. In the final year of this post-juvenile moult, the remaining primaries are moulted and all the juvenile feathers will have been replaced. Another moult takes place during years six to twelve of the bird's life. This happens between June and October after the conclusion of the breeding season and again it is a staged process with six to nine main flight feathers being replaced each year. Such a moulting pattern lasting several years is repeated throughout the bird's life.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl is a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">Bubo</a></i>, which may include either 22 or 25 extant species. Almost all the larger owl species in the world today are included in <i>Bubo</i>. Based on an extensive fossil record and a central distribution of extant species on that continent, the <i>Bubo</i> appears to have evolved into existence in Africa, although early radiations seem to branch from southern Asia as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Two genera belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl">scops owls</a> complex, the <a href="/wiki/Giant_scops_owl" title="Giant scops owl">giant scops owls</a> (<i>Otus gurneyi</i>) found in Asia and the <i><a href="/wiki/Ptilopsis" title="Ptilopsis">Ptilopsis</a></i> or the white-faced scops owl found in Africa, although firmly ensconced in the scops owl group, appear to share some characteristics with the eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> is also related to the <i>Bubo</i> and is considered a "sister complex", with the <i><a href="/wiki/Pulsatrix" title="Pulsatrix">Pulsatrix</a></i> possibly being intermediate between the two.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl appears to represent an expansion of the genus <i>Bubo</i> into the Eurasian continent. A few of the other species of <i>Bubo</i> seem to have been derived from the Eurasian eagle-owl, making it a "<a href="/wiki/Paraspecies" title="Paraspecies">paraspecies</a>", or they at least share a relatively recent common ancestor.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Pharaoh eagle-owl, distributed in the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and sections of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a> through <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> where rocky outcrops are found, was until recently considered a subspecies of the Eurasian eagle-owl. It appears that the Pharaoh eagle-owl differs about 3.8% in <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> from the Eurasian eagle-owl, well past the minimum genetic difference to differentiate species of 1.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Smaller and paler than Eurasian eagle-owls, the Pharaoh eagle-owl can also be considered a distinct species largely due to its higher pitched and more descending call and the observation that Eurasian eagle-owls formerly found in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> (<i>B. b. hispanus</i>) apparently did not breed with the co-existing Pharaoh eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, the race still found together with the Pharaoh eagle-owl in the wild (<i>B. b. interpositus</i>) in the central <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> has been found to interbreed in the wild with the Pharaoh eagle-owl, although genetical materials have indicated <i>interpositus</i> may itself be a distinct species from the Eurasian eagle-owl, as it differs from the nominate subspecies of the Eurasian eagle-owl by 2.8% in mitochondrial DNA.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Indian eagle-owl (<i>B. bengalensis</i>) was also considered a subspecies of the Eurasian eagle-owl until recently, but its smaller size, distinct voice (more clipped and high-pitched than the Eurasian), and the fact that it is largely <a href="/wiki/Allopatric_speciation" title="Allopatric speciation">allopatric</a> in distribution (filling out the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>) with other Eurasian eagle-owl races has led to it being considered a distinct species.<sup id="cite_ref-Sibley_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sibley-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The mitochondrial DNA of the Indian species also appears considerably distinct from the Eurasian species.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cape_eagle-owl" title="Cape eagle-owl">Cape eagle-owl</a> (<i>B. capensis</i>) appears to represent a return of this genetic line back into the African continent, where it leads a lifestyle similar to Eurasian eagle-owls albeit far to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Another offshoot of the northern <i>Bubo</i> group is the snowy owl. It appears to have separated from other <i>Bubo</i> at least 4 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Potapov_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potapov-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The fourth and most famous derivation of the evolutionary line that includes the Eurasian eagle-owl is the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a>, which appears to have been the result of primitive eagle-owls spreading into <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It has been stated that the great horned owls and Eurasian eagle-owls are barely distinct as species, with a similar level of divergence in their plumages as the Eurasian and North American representations of the great grey owl or the long-eared owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> There are more outward physical differences between the great horned owl and the Eurasian eagle-owl than in those two examples, including a great size difference favoring the Eurasian species, the great horned owl’s horizontal rather than vertical underside barring, yellow rather than orange eyes and a much strong black bracket to the facial disc, not to mention a number of differences in their reproductive behaviour and distinctive voices.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Furthermore, genetic research has revealed that the snowy owl is more closely related to the great horned owl than are Eurasian eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The most closely related species beyond the Pharaoh, Indian and Cape eagle-owls to the Eurasian eagle-owl is the smaller, less powerful and African <a href="/wiki/Spotted_eagle-owl" title="Spotted eagle-owl">spotted eagle-owl</a> (<i>B. africanus</i>), which was likely to have divided from the line before they radiated away from Africa. Somehow, genetic materials indicate the spotted eagle-owl appears to share a more recent ancestor with the Indian eagle-owl than with the Eurasian eagle-owl or even the <a href="/wiki/Sympatry" title="Sympatry">sympatric</a> Cape eagle-owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Eurasian eagle-owls in captivity have produced apparently healthy hybrids with both the Indian eagle-owl and the great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The pharaoh, Indian and Cape eagle-owls and the great horned owl are all broadly similar in size to each other, but all are considerably smaller than the Eurasian eagle-owl, which averages at least 15–30% larger in linear dimensions and 30–50% larger in body mass than these other related species, possibly as the eagle-owls adapted to warmer climates and smaller prey. Fossils from southern France have indicated that during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a>, Eurasian eagle-owls (this paleosubspecies is given the name <i>B. b. davidi</i>) were larger than they are today, even larger were those found in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> (either <i>B. b. bignadensis</i> or <i>B. bignadensis</i>), which were deemed to date to the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> About 12 <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are recognized today.<sup id="cite_ref-AviBase_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AviBase-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG/170px-Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG/255px-Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG/340px-Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3840" data-file-height="5760" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_sibiricus_-_01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive adult eagle-owl, although identified as part of the subspecies <i>B. b. sibiricus</i>, its appearance is more consistent with <i>B. b. ruthenus</i>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tierpark_Berlin,_Bubo_bubo_omissus,_252-357.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG/170px-Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG/255px-Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG/340px-Tierpark_Berlin%2C_Bubo_bubo_omissus%2C_252-357.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2520" data-file-height="3776" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tierpark_Berlin,_Bubo_bubo_omissus,_252-357.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>B. b. omissus</i> at <a href="/wiki/Tierpark_Berlin" title="Tierpark Berlin">Tierpark Berlin</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>B. b. bubo</i> (Linnaeus, 1758) – Also known as the <b>European eagle-owl</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> the nominate subspecies inhabits continental Europe from near the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> in Norway, Sweden, Finland, the southern <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arkhangelsk" title="Arkhangelsk">Arkhangelsk</a> where it ranges north to about latitude 640 30' N., southward to the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, central Germany, to southeastern Belgium, eastern, central, and southern France to Italy and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, and through Central and <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">Southeastern Europe</a> to <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>. It intergrades with <i>B. b. ruthenus</i> in northern Russia around the basin of the upper <a href="/wiki/Mezen_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Mezen River">Mezen River</a> and in the eastern vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Gorki_Leninskiye" title="Gorki Leninskiye">Gorki Leninskiye</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tambov" title="Tambov">Tambov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voronezh" title="Voronezh">Voronezh</a>, and intergrades with <i>B. b. interpositus</i> in northern Ukraine.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This is a medium-sized race, measuring in wing chord length 435–480&#160;mm (17.1–18.9&#160;in) in males and 455–500&#160;mm (17.9–19.7&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In captive owls of this subspecies, the mean wingspan were 157&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 2&#160;in) for males and 167.5&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 6&#160;in) for females.<sup id="cite_ref-OwlBreederWings_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OwlBreederWings-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The total bill length is 45 to 56&#160;mm (1.8 to 2.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Adult male European eagle-owls from Norway weigh 1.63 to 2.81&#160;kg (3.6 to 6.2&#160;lb), averaging 2.38&#160;kg (5.2&#160;lb), while females there weigh from 2.28 to 4.2&#160;kg (5.0 to 9.3&#160;lb), averaging 2.95&#160;kg (6.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Unsurprisingly, adult owls from western Finland were about the same size, averaging 2.65&#160;kg (5.8&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola2_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola2-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> The subspecies seems to follow <a href="/wiki/Bergmann%E2%80%99s_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Bergmann’s rule">Bergmann’s rule</a> in regards to body size decreasing closer to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>, as specimens from <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a> average 2.14 or 2.3&#160;kg (4.7 or 5.1&#160;lb) in body mass and those from Italy average about 2.01&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> The weight range for eagle-owls in Italy is 1.5 to 3&#160;kg (3.3 to 6.6&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The nominate subspecies is perhaps the darkest and most richly coloured of eagle-owl subspecies. Many nominate birds are heavily overlaid with broad black streaking over the upper-parts, head and chest. While generally a brownish base-colour, many nominate owls can appear rich rufous, especially about the head, upper-back and wing primaries. The lower belly is usually a buff brown, as opposed to whitish or yellowish in several other subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Birds seen from southern Italy and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> may show a tendency to be smaller than more northern birds and reportedly are duller, possessing paler ground coloration, and more narrow streaks, but museum specimens are often not hugely distinct from north Italian eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, some birds are so darkly plumaged as to give a blackish-brown impression with almost no paler colour showing.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. hispanus</i> (Rothschild and Hartert, 1910) – Also known as the <b>Spanish eagle-owl</b> or the <b>Iberian eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies mainly occurs on the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, where it occupies a majority of Spain and scattered spots in Portugal.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. hispanus</i> at least historically occurred in wooded areas of the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, making it the only subspecies of Eurasian eagle-owl known to breed in Africa, but this population is thought to be extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This is a fairly small-bodied subspecies. In males, wing chord length can range from 400 to 450&#160;mm (16 to 18&#160;in) and in females from 445 to 485&#160;mm (17.5 to 19.1&#160;in). Wingspans in this subspecies can vary from 131 to 168&#160;cm (4&#160;ft 4&#160;in to 5&#160;ft 6&#160;in), averaging about 154.1&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 1&#160;in). Among standard measurements of <i>B. b. hispanus</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> is 229 to 310&#160;mm (9.0 to 12.2&#160;in), the total bill length is 38.9 to 54.3&#160;mm (1.53 to 2.14&#160;in) and the <a href="/wiki/Tarsus_(skeleton)" title="Tarsus (skeleton)">tarsus</a> is 64.5 to 81&#160;mm (2.54 to 3.19&#160;in). Adult male <i>B. b. hispanus</i> from Spain weigh 1.22 to 1.9&#160;kg (2.7 to 4.2&#160;lb), averaging 1.63&#160;kg (3.6&#160;lb), while females weigh from 1.75 to 2.49&#160;kg (3.9 to 5.5&#160;lb), averaging 2.11&#160;kg (4.7&#160;lb). In terms of its life history, this may be the most extensively studied subspecies of eagle-owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The Spanish eagle-owl is the most similar in plumage to the nominate subspecies amongst other subspecies, but tends to be a somewhat lighter, more greyish colour, with generally lighter streaking and a paler belly.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. ruthenus</i> (Buturlin and Zhitkov, 1906)- May be also known as the <b>eastern eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies replaces the nominate in eastern Russia from about latitude 660 N. in the <a href="/wiki/Timan-Pechora_Basin" title="Timan-Pechora Basin">Timan-Pechora Basin</a> south to the western <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> and the upper <a href="/wiki/Don_River_(Russia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Don River (Russia)">Don</a> and lower <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Volga River">Volga Rivers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This is a fairly large subspecies going on wing chord length, which is 430–468&#160;mm (16.9–18.4&#160;in) in males and 470–515&#160;mm (18.5–20.3&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The subspecies is intermediate in coloration between the nominate subspecies and <i>B. b. sibiricus</i>. <i>B. b. ruthenus</i> may be confused with <i>B. b. interpositus</i>, even by authoritative ornithologists. <i>B. b. interpositus</i> is darker than <i>B. b. ruthenus</i>, distinctly more yellowish, less gray, and its brown pattern is darker, heavier, and more regular. The entire colour pattern of <i>B. b. interpositus</i> is brighter, richer, and more contrasting than that of <i>B. b. ruthenus</i>, but <i>B. b. interpositus</i>, though very well characterized, is an intermediate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG/220px-Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG/330px-Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG/440px-Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eagle_Owl_IMG_9203.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In San Francisco Zoo</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>B. b. interpositus</i> (Rothschild and Hartert, 1910)- May be also known as <b>Aharoni’s eagle-owl</b> or the <b>Byzantine eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. interpositus</i> ranges from southern Russia, south of the nominate, with which it intergrades in northern <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Bessarabia" title="Bessarabia">Bessarabia</a> and the steppes of the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> north to <a href="/wiki/Kyiv" title="Kyiv">Kyiv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kharkiv" title="Kharkiv">Kharkiv</a> then eastward to the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transcaucasia" title="Transcaucasia">Transcaucasia</a> to northwestern and northern Iran (<a href="/wiki/Elburz" class="mw-redirect" title="Elburz">Elburz</a>, region of <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, and probably the southern <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian</a> districts), and through <a href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" class="mw-redirect" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> south to <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> but not to the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_desert" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian desert">Syrian desert</a> where it is replaced by the pharaoh eagle-owl. The latter and <i>B. b. interpositus</i> reportedly hybridize from western <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> south to southern <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. interpositus</i> may be a distinct species from the Eurasian eagle-owl based on genetic studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> This medium-sized subspecies is about the same size as the nominate subspecies <i>B. b. bubo</i>, with male wing chord lengths 425 to 475&#160;mm (16.7 to 18.7&#160;in) and female lengths of 440 to 503&#160;mm (17.3 to 19.8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> It differs from the nominate subspecies by being paler and more yellow, less ferruginous, and by having a sharper brown pattern; from <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> by being very much darker and less yellow, and also by being much more sharply and heavily patterned with brown. Aharoni’s eagle-owl is darker and more rusty than <i>B. b. ruthenus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg/220px-White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg/330px-White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg/440px-White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="4928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:White_horned_owl_portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive adult eagle-owl with a pale appearance, likely part of <i>B. b. sibiricus</i>.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><i>B. b. sibiricus</i> (Gloger, 1833)- Also known as the <b>western Siberian eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is distributed from the <a href="/wiki/Ural_Mountains" title="Ural Mountains">Ural Mountains</a> of western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bashkortostan" title="Bashkortostan">Bashkiria</a> to the mid <a href="/wiki/Ob_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ob River">Ob River</a> and the western <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>, north to limits of the taiga, the most northerly distribution known in the species overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. sibiricus</i> is a large subspecies, wherein the males measure 435–480&#160;mm (17.1–18.9&#160;in) in wing chord length, while the females are 472–515&#160;mm (18.6–20.3&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Captive males were found to measure 155 to 170&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 1&#160;in to 5&#160;ft 7&#160;in) in wingspan and weigh 1.62 to 3.2&#160;kg (3.6 to 7.1&#160;lb); whereas the females measure 165 to 190&#160;cm (5&#160;ft 5&#160;in to 6&#160;ft 3&#160;in) in wingspan and weigh 2.28 to 4.5&#160;kg (5.0 to 9.9&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-OwlBreederWings_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OwlBreederWings-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Males were cited with a mean body mass of approximately 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is physically the most distinctive of all the Eurasian eagle-owls, and is sometimes considered the most "beautiful and striking".<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It is the most pale of the eagle-owl subspecies; the general coloration is a buffy off-white overlaid with dark markings. The crown, hindneck and underparts are streaked blackish but somewhat sparingly, with the lower breast and belly indistinctly barred, the primary coverts dark, contrasting with rest of the wing. The head, back and shoulders are only somewhat dark unlike in most other subspecies. In the eastern limits of its range, <i>B. b. sibiricus</i> may intergrade with <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. yenisseensis</i> (Buturlin, 1911)- Also known as the <b>eastern Siberian eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is found in central Siberia from about the <a href="/wiki/Ob_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ob River">Ob</a> eastward to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>, north to about latitudes 580 to 590 N on the <a href="/wiki/Yenisei_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Yenisei River">Yenisei River</a>, south to the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarbagatai_Mountains" title="Tarbagatai Mountains">Tarbagatai</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saur_Mountains" title="Saur Mountains">Saur Mountain</a> ranges and in <a href="/wiki/Tuvan_People%27s_Republic" title="Tuvan People&#39;s Republic">Tannu Tuva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khangai_Mountains" title="Khangai Mountains">Khangai Mountains</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, grading into <i>B. b. sibiricus</i> near <a href="/wiki/Tomsk" title="Tomsk">Tomsk</a> in the west and into <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> in the east of northern Mongolia. The zone of intergradations with the latter in Mongolia seems to be quite extensive, with intermediate eagle-owls being especially prevalent around the <a href="/wiki/Tuul_River" title="Tuul River">Tuul River Valley</a>, resulting in owls intermediate in coloration between <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i> and <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i> is a large subspecies, with wing chord lengths of 435–470&#160;mm (17.1–18.5&#160;in) in males and 473–518&#160;mm (18.6–20.4&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i> is typically much darker with more yellowish ground colour than <i>B. b. sibiricus</i>. It does have a similar amount of dazzling white on its underwing as does <i>sibiricus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> It is buffy-greyish overall with well-expressed dark patterning on the upper-parts and around the head. The underside is overall pale greyish with black streaking.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. jakutensis</i> (Buturlin, 1908)- May be also known as the <b>Yakutian eagle-owl</b>. This subspecies inhabits northeastern Siberia, from southern <a href="/wiki/Sakha_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Sakha Republic">Yakutia</a> north to about latitude 640 N., west in the basin of the <a href="/wiki/Vilyuy_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Vilyuy River">Vilyuy River</a> to the upper <a href="/wiki/Nizhnyaya_Tunguska_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Nizhnyaya Tunguska River">Nizhnyaya Tunguska River</a>, and east to the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a> from <a href="/wiki/Magadan" title="Magadan">Magadan</a> south to the <a href="/wiki/Khabarovsk_Krai" title="Khabarovsk Krai">Khabarovsk Krai</a>. It has been reported farther north, from the regions of the upper <a href="/wiki/Kolyma_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Kolyma River">Kolyma River</a> and the upper <a href="/wiki/Chukotka_Autonomous_Okrug" title="Chukotka Autonomous Okrug">Anadyr</a>. Eurasian eagle-owls are absent in <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> and north of the <a href="/wiki/Verkhoyansk_Range" title="Verkhoyansk Range">Verkhoyansk Range</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This is a large subspecies, rivaling the proceeding two subspecies as the largest of all eagle-owls, going on wing chord length, which subspecies is largest is unclear considering the extensive size overlap in wing size. The wing chord is 455 to 490&#160;mm (17.9 to 19.3&#160;in) in males and 480 to 503&#160;mm (18.9 to 19.8&#160;in) in females.<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. jakutensis</i> is much darker and browner above than both <i>B. b. sibiricus</i> and <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i>, though its coloration is more diffused, less sharp than the latter. It is more distinctly streaked and barred below than <i>B. b. sibiricus</i> while being whiter and more heavily vermiculated below than <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> (Poljakov, 1915) – Would presumably be also known as the <b>Ussuri eagle-owl</b>. This subspecies ranges from southeastern Siberia, to the south of the range of <i>B. b. jakutensis</i>, southward through eastern <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amur_Oblast" title="Amur Oblast">Amurland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Ukraine" title="Green Ukraine">Ussuriland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchurian</a> portion of the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese provinces</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is also reportedly found in the southern <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands" title="Kuril Islands">Kuril Islands</a> ranging down to as far as northern <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, the only Japanese representation in the Eurasian eagle-owl species, although this is apparently not a stable, viable population.<sup id="cite_ref-Yuko_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yuko-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Going on wing chord length, <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> is slightly smaller than the various subspecies from further north in Siberia. Males have a wing chord length of 430–475&#160;mm (16.9–18.7&#160;in) and females are 460–502&#160;mm (18.1–19.8&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies differs from <i>B. b. jakutensis</i> by being much darker throughout. It is also darker than <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i>. The brown markings on the upper parts of <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> are much more extensive and diffused than in <i>B. b. jakutensis</i> or <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i>, with the result that the white markings are much less conspicuous in <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> than in the other two subspecies. The under parts are also more buffy, much less white, and more heavily streaked and vermiculated in <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i> than in the two more northerly, larger subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It overlaps considerably with <i>jakutensis</i> and some birds are of an intermediate appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-Yuko_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yuko-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. turcomanus</i> (Eversmann, 1835)- Also known as the <b>steppe eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> It is distributed from <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Volga_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Volga River">Volga</a> and upper <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural River">Ural Rivers</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> coast and the former <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a>, but replaced in that country by <i>B. b. omissus</i> in the mountainous south and in the coastal region of the <a href="/wiki/Mangyshlak_Peninsula" title="Mangyshlak Peninsula">Mangyshlak Peninsula</a> by <i>B. b. gladkovi</i>. Out of Kazakhstan, the range of <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> continues through the <a href="/wiki/Transbaikal" title="Transbaikal">Transbaikal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> to western <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies appears to be variable in size, but is generally medium-sized. Males can range in wing chord length from 418–468&#160;mm (16.5–18.4&#160;in) and females from 440 to 512&#160;mm (17.3 to 20.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In standard measurements, the tail is 260–310&#160;mm (10–12&#160;in), the tarsus is 77–81&#160;mm (3.0–3.2&#160;in) and the bill is 45–47&#160;mm (1.8–1.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies can reportedly weigh from 1.5 to 3.8&#160;kg (3.3 to 8.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> The plumage background colour is pale, yellowish-buff. The dark patterns on the upper- and underparts is paler, less well-defined and more shattered than in <i>B. b. interpositus</i>. Dark longitudinal patterning on the under-parts discontinue above the belly. <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> is greyer than <i>B. b. hemalachanus</i> but is otherwise somewhat similar-looking. This subspecies is unique in that it seems to shun mountainous and obvious rocky habitats in favor of low hills, plateaus, lowlands, steppes, and semideserts at or near sea-level.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. omissus</i> (Dementiev, 1932) – May be also known as the <b>Turkoman eagle-owl</b> or the <b>Turkmenian eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. omissus</i> is native to <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and adjacent regions of northeastern Iran and western <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This is a small subspecies (only <i>nikolskii</i> averages smaller among currently accepted races), with males possessing a wing chord length of 404–450&#160;mm (15.9–17.7&#160;in) and females of 425 to 460&#160;mm (16.7 to 18.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. omissus</i> may be considered a typical sub-desert form. The general coloration is an ochre to buffy off-yellow; with the dark pattern on the upper- and under-parts being relatively undefined. The dark shaft-streaks on nape are very narrow, while the dark longitudinal patterning on the underparts does not cover the belly. A dark cross-pattern on the belly and flanks is thinner and paler than in <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> and some individuals may appear almost all pale below. Compared to <i>B. b. nikolskii</i>, which may occupy the more southern reaches of the same upland ranges, it is somewhat larger as well as darker, less distinctly yellowish and more heavily streaked.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. nikolskii</i> (Zarudny, 1905) – May be also known in English as either the <b>Afghan eagle-owl</b> or the <b>Iranian eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The range of <i>B. b. nikolskii</i> appears to extend from the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Mountains" title="Balkan Mountains">Balkan Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kopet_Dagh" class="mw-redirect" title="Kopet Dagh">Kopet Dagh</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Transcaspia" class="mw-redirect" title="Transcaspia">Transcaspia</a> eastward to southeastern <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> or to perhaps southwestern <a href="/wiki/Tadzhikistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tadzhikistan">Tadzhikistan</a>, then southward 290 N. It may range north to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baluchistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Baluchistan">Baluchistan</a> south to the region of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Qal%27et_al-Bahrain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Qal&#39;et al-Bahrain (page does not exist)">Kalat</a>, or at about latitude of <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a>. In Iran, <i>B. b. nikolskii</i> is replaced by <i>B. b. interpositus</i> in the north, and probably also in the northwest, and probably by <i>B. b. hemalachana</i> in <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan" title="Badakhshan">Badakhshan</a>, part of northeastern Afghanistan. The birds of southern <a href="/wiki/Tadzhikistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tadzhikistan">Tadzhikistan</a> found west of the <a href="/wiki/Pamirs" class="mw-redirect" title="Pamirs">Pamirs</a> are more or less intermediate between <i>B. b. omissus</i> and <i>B. b. hemachalana</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This is the smallest known subspecies of eagle-owl, though the only known measurements have been of wing chord length. Males can measure 378 to 430&#160;mm (14.9 to 16.9&#160;in) and females can measure 410 to 465&#160;mm (16.1 to 18.3&#160;in) in wing chord.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Other than its smaller size, <i>B. b. nikolskii</i> is distinguished from the somewhat similar <i>B. b. omissus</i> by its rusty wash and being less dark above.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rothschild_33-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rothschild-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. hemachalana</i> (Hume, 1873) – Also known as the <b>Himalayan eagle-owl</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The range of <i>B. b. hemachalana</i> extends from the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, westward to the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> system in <a href="/wiki/Russian_Turkestan" title="Russian Turkestan">Russian Turkestan</a>, west to the <a href="/wiki/Karatau,_Kazakhstan" title="Karatau, Kazakhstan">Karatau</a>, north to the <a href="/wiki/Dzungarian_Alatau" title="Dzungarian Alatau">Dzungarian Alatau</a>, east to at least the <a href="/wiki/Beylik_of_Teke" title="Beylik of Teke">Tekkes Valley</a> in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, and south to the regions of <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yarkant_County" title="Yarkant County">Yarkant</a> and probably the western <a href="/wiki/Kunlun_Mountains" title="Kunlun Mountains">Kunlun Mountains</a>. This bird is partly migratory, descending to the plains of <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> with colder winter weather, and apparently reaches northern <a href="/wiki/Balochistan" title="Balochistan">Balochistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This is a medium-sized subspecies, though it is larger than other potentially abutting arid Asian eagle-owl subspecies, which share a somewhat similar yellowish ground colour. The male attains a wing chord length of 420–485&#160;mm (16.5–19.1&#160;in), while the female’s wing chord is 450–505&#160;mm (17.7–19.9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The bill measures 42–45&#160;mm (1.7–1.8&#160;in) in length. 11 adult eagle-owls of the subspecies from the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> averaged 301&#160;mm (11.9&#160;in) in tail length, 78&#160;mm (3.1&#160;in) in tarsus length and scaled an average of 2.16&#160;kg (4.8&#160;lb) in mass.<sup id="cite_ref-Qinghu_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qinghu-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> This subspecies is physically similar to <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> but the background colour is more light yellowish-brown and less buff. The dark patterns on the upperparts and underparts are more expressed and less regular than in <i>B. b. turcomanus</i> and <i>B. b. omissus</i> and the general colour from the mantle to the ear tufts is a more consistent brownish colour than most other abutting races. <i>B. b. hemachalana</i> differs from <i>B. b. yenisseensis</i> by being much more yellow on the rump, under tail coverts, and outer tail feathers, rather than grayish or whitish, and the ground coloration of its body is more yellowish above, and is less whitish below. Dark longitudinal pattern on the under-parts cover the forebelly.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. kiautschensis</i> (Reichenow, 1903) – This subspecies could be also known as the <b>North Chinese eagle-owl</b>.
