It ranges from <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> and China, south of the range of <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i>, southward to <a href="/wiki/Kwangtung" class="mw-redirect" title="Kwangtung">Kwangtung</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>, and inland to <a href="/wiki/Szechwan" class="mw-redirect" title="Szechwan">Szechwan</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Kansu" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansu">Kansu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> This is a smallish subspecies, with the male’s wing chord measuring 410–448&#160;mm (16.1–17.6&#160;in) and the female’s being 440–485&#160;mm (17.3–19.1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie2_34-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, this subspecies was found to average 2.26&#160;kg (5.0&#160;lb) in mass, with a range of 1.8 to 2.9&#160;kg (4.0 to 6.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> <i>B. b. kiautschensis</i> is much darker, more tawny and rufous, and slightly smaller than <i>B. b. ussuriensis</i>. According to museum accounts, it resembles the nominate subspecies from Europe (though obviously considerably disparate in distribution) rather closely in coloration but differs from it by being paler, more mottled, and less heavily marked with brown on the upper parts, by having narrower dark shaft streaks on the under parts, which average also duller and more ocher, and by averaging smaller.<sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Images from South Korea of captive and wild owls show, on the contrary, that this race may be easily as darkly marked as most nominate eagle-owls with a more rufous base colour altogether suggesting a richer and more dusky-colored eagle-owl than from almost any other population.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>B. b. swinhoei</i> (Hartert, 1913) – This subspecies could be also known as the <b>South Chinese eagle-owl</b>. It is endemic to southeastern China. A quite rufescent form, it is somewhat similar to <i>B. b. kiautschensis</i>. In this smallish subspecies, the wing chord measures 410–465&#160;mm (16.1–18.3&#160;in) in both sexes. This is a rather poorly known and described subspecies and is considered invalid by some authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weick_9-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weick-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vaurie_32-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vaurie-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg/220px-Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg/330px-Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg/440px-Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1537" data-file-height="1601" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_winter_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian eagle-owls are frequently at home in harsh wintery areas.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubro_(Bubo_bubo).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG/220px-Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG/330px-Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG/440px-Hubro_%28Bubo_bubo%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2661" data-file-height="2477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubro_(Bubo_bubo).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eagle-owls often prefer areas with dense conifers for seclusion.</div></div></div>
<p>Eagle-owls are distributed somewhat sparsely but can potentially inhabit a wide range of habitats, with a partiality for <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">irregular topography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> They have been found in habitats as diverse as northern <a href="/wiki/Coniferous" class="mw-redirect" title="Coniferous">coniferous</a> forests to the <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">edge</a> of vast <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Essentially, Eurasian eagle-owls have been found living in almost every climatic and environmental condition on the Eurasian continent, excluding the greatest extremities, i.e. they are absent from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">humid rainforest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, as well as the high <a href="/wiki/Arctic_tundra" class="mw-redirect" title="Arctic tundra">Arctic tundra</a>, both of which they are more or less replaced by other variety of <i>Bubo</i> owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> They are often found in the largest numbers in areas where <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliffs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ravine" title="Ravine">ravines</a> are surrounded by a scattering of <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bush_(plant)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bush (plant)">bushes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">Grassland</a> areas such as <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">alpine meadows</a> or <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> can also host them so long as they have the cover and protection of rocky areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The preference of eagle-owls for places with irregular topography has been reported in most known studies. The obvious benefit of such nesting locations is that both nests and daytime roosts located in rocky areas and/or steep slopes would be less accessible to predators, including man. Also, they may be attracted to the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> areas, due to the fact that the soft soil of wet areas is conducive to burrowing by the small, terrestrial mammals normally preferred in the diet, such as <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">rabbits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to their preference for rocky areas, the species is often found in <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystems" title="Montane ecosystems">mountainous areas</a> and can be found up to elevations of 2,100&#160;m (6,900&#160;ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a> and 4,500&#160;m (14,800&#160;ft) in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and 4,700&#160;m (15,400&#160;ft) in the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Qinghu_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Qinghu-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> They can also be found living at sea level and may nest amongst rocky sea cliffs.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Despite their success in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">sub-arctic zones</a> and mountains that are frigid for much of the year, warmer conditions seem to result in more successful breeding attempts per studies in the <a href="/wiki/Eifel" title="Eifel">Eifel</a> region of Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Dalbeck_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalbeck-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In a study from Spain, areas primarily consisting of woodlands (52% of study area being forested) were preferred with <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pine</a> trees predominating the <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oaks</a> in habitats used, as opposed to truly mixed <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">pine-oak woodland</a>. Pine and other coniferous stands are often preferred in great horned owls as well due to the constant density, which make it more likely to overlook the large birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In mountainous forest, they are not generally found in <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">enclosed wooded areas</a>, as is the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>), instead usually near <a href="/wiki/Woodland_edge" title="Woodland edge">forest edge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Only 2.7% of the habitat included in the territorial ranges for eagle-owls per the habitat study in Spain consisted of <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">cultivated</a> or <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_land" title="Agricultural land">agricultural land</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, compared to <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a>, they can visit cultivated land more regularly in hunting forays due to their nocturnal habits, which allows them to largely evade human activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In the Italian <a href="/wiki/Alps" title="Alps">Alps</a>, it was found that almost no pristine habitat remained and locally eagle-owls nested in the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Town" title="Town">towns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ski_resort" title="Ski resort">ski resorts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg/220px-Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg/330px-Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg/440px-Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg 2x" data-file-width="864" data-file-height="568" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubi_huuhkaja.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An eagle-owl at the stadium in Helsinki</div></div></div>
<p>Although found in the largest numbers in areas sparsely populated by humans, farmland is sometimes inhabited and they even have been observed living in <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">park-like</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Peri-urbanisation" title="Peri-urbanisation">quiet</a> settings within European cities.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Since 2005, at least five pairs have nested in <a href="/wiki/Helsinki" title="Helsinki">Helsinki</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This is due in part to feral <a href="/wiki/European_rabbit" title="European rabbit">European rabbits</a> (<i>Oryctolagus cuniculus</i>) having recently populated the Helsinki area, originally from pet rabbits released to the wild. The number is expected to increase due to the growth of the European rabbit population in Helsinki. <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>), the often preferred prey species by biomass of the eagle-owls in their natural habitat, live only in rural areas of Finland, not in the city centre. In June 2007, an eagle-owl nicknamed 'Bubi' landed in the crowded <a href="/wiki/Helsinki_Olympic_Stadium" title="Helsinki Olympic Stadium">Helsinki Olympic Stadium</a> during the European Football Championship qualification match between Finland and Belgium. The match was interrupted for six minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-hs_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hs-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> After tiring of the match, following Jonathan Johansson's opening goal for Finland, the bird left the scene.<sup id="cite_ref-hs_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hs-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Finland_national_football_team" title="Finland national football team">Finland's national football team</a> have had the nickname <i>Huuhkajat</i> (Finnish for "Eurasian eagle-owls") ever since. The owl was named "Helsinki Citizen of the Year" in December 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg/220px-1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg/330px-1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg/440px-1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="926" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1Puchacz_obronna_poza.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Threat posture</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl is largely nocturnal in activity, as are most owl species, with its activity focused in the first few hours after sunset and the last few hours before sunrise.<sup id="cite_ref-Delgado,_M._D._M._2007_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delgado,_M._D._M._2007-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In the northern stretches of its range, partial diurnal behaviour has been recorded, including active hunting in broad daylight during the late afternoon. In such areas, full nightfall is essentially non-existent at the peak of summer, so eagle-owls must presumably hunt and actively brood at the nest during daylight.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl has a number of vocalizations that are used at different times.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It will usually select obvious topographic features such as rocky pinnacles, stark ridges and mountain peaks to use as regular song posts. These are dotted along the outer edges of the eagle-owl's territory and they are visited often but only for a few minutes at a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vocal activity is almost entirely confined to the colder months from late fall through winter, with vocal activity in October through December mainly having territorial purposes and from January to February being primarily oriented towards courtship and mating purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani2_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The territorial song, which can be heard at great distance, is a deep resonant <i>ooh-hu</i> with emphasis on the first syllable for the male, and a more high-pitched and slightly more drawn-out <i>uh-hu</i> for the female.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> It is not uncommon for a pair to perform an antiphonal duet. The widely used name in Germany as well as some other sections of Europe for this species is <i>uhu</i> due to its song. At 250–350&#160;Hz, the Eurasian eagle-owls territorial song or call is deeper, farther-carrying and is often considering "more impressive" than the territorial songs of the great horned owl or even that of the slightly larger Blakiston's fish owl, although the horned owl’s call averages slightly longer in duration.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Other calls include a rather faint, laughter-like <i>OO-OO-oo</i> and a harsh <i>kveck-kveck</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Intruding eagle-owls and other potential dangers may be met with a "terrifying", extremely loud <i>hooo</i>. Raucous barks not unlike those of ural owls or long-eared owls have been recorded but are deeper and more powerful than those species’ barks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Annoyance at close quarters is expressed by bill-clicking and cat-like spitting, and a defensive posture involves lowering the head, ruffling the back feathers, fanning the tail and spreading the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Uhu_im_Flug.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Uhu_im_Flug.jpg/220px-Uhu_im_Flug.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Uhu_im_Flug.jpg/330px-Uhu_im_Flug.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Uhu_im_Flug.jpg/440px-Uhu_im_Flug.jpg 2x" data-file-width="711" data-file-height="533" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Uhu_im_Flug.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian eagle-owls are subject to frequent mobbing by <a href="/wiki/Carrion_crow" title="Carrion crow">crows</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl rarely assumes the so-called "tall-thin position", which is when an owl adopts an upright stance with plumage closely compressed and may stand tightly beside a tree trunk. Among others, the <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> is among the most often reported to sit with this pose.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The great horned owl has been more regularly recorded using the tall-thin, if not as consistently as some <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i> owls, and it is commonly thought to aid camouflage if encountering a threatening or novel animal or sound.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl is a broad-winged species and engages in a strong, direct flight, usually consisting of shallow wing beats and long, surprisingly fast glides. It has, unusually for an owl, also been known to soar on updrafts on rare occasions. The latter method of flight has led them to be mistaken for <i><a href="/wiki/Buteo" title="Buteo">Buteos</a></i>, which are smaller and quite differently proportioned.<sup id="cite_ref-peregrinefund.org_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peregrinefund.org-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Usually when seen flying during the day, it is due to being disturbed by humans or <a href="/wiki/Mobbing_(animal_behavior)" title="Mobbing (animal behavior)">mobbing</a> <a href="/wiki/Crow" title="Crow">crows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Eurasian eagle-owls are highly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">sedentary</a>, normally maintaining a single <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territory</a> throughout their adult lives.
</p><p>Even those near the northern limits of their range, where winters are harsh and likely to bear little in food, the eagle-owl does not leave its native range. There are cases from Russia of Eurasian eagle-owls moving south for the winter, as the <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">icebound</a>, infamously harsh climate there is too severe even for these hardy birds and their prey. Similarly, Eurasian eagle-owls living in the Tibetan highlands and Himalayas may in some cases vacate their normal territories when winter hits and move south. Even in those two examples, there is no evidence of consistent, annual migration by Eurasian eagle-owls and the birds may eke out a living on their normal territories even in the sparsest times.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dietary_biology_of_the_Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Dietary biology of the Eurasian eagle-owl">Dietary biology of the Eurasian eagle-owl</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Eurasian eagle-owls are strictly territorial and will defend their territories from interloping eagle-owls year around, but territorial calling appears to peak around October to early January.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani2_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Territory size is similar or occasionally slightly greater than great horned owl: averaging 15 to 80&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (5.8 to 30.9&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Territories are established by the male eagle-owl, who selected the highest points in the territory from which to sing. The high prominence of singing perches allows their song to be heard at greater distances and lessens the need for potentially dangerous physical confrontations in the areas where territories may meet.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani2_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Nearly as important in territorial behaviour as vocalization is the white throat patch. When taxidermied specimens with flared white throats were placed around the perimeter of eagle-owl territories, male eagle-owls reacted quite strongly and often attacked the stuffed owl, reacting more mildly to a stuffed eagle-owl with a non-flared white throat. Females were less likely to be aggressive to mounted specimens and did not seem to vary in their response whether exposed to the specimens with or without the puffed up white patch.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In January and February, the primary function for vocalization becomes for the purpose of courtship.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani2_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani2-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> More often than not, eagle-owls will pair for life but usually engage in courtship rituals annually, most likely to re-affirm pair bonds.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> When calling for the purposes of courtship, males tend to bow and hoot loudly but do so in a less contorted manner than the male great horned owl.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Courtship in the Eurasian eagle-owl may involve bouts of "duetting", with the male sitting upright and the female bowing as she calls. There may be mutual bowing, billing and fondling before the female flies to a perch where <a href="/wiki/Copulation_(zoology)" title="Copulation (zoology)">coitus</a> occurs, usually taking place several times over the course of a few minutes.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The male selects breeding sites and advertises their potential to the female by flying to them and kneading out a small depression (if soil is present) and making staccato notes and clucking noises. Several potential sites may be presented, with the female selecting one.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Like all owls, Eurasian eagle-owls do not build nests or add material but nest on the surface or material already present. Eurasian eagle-owls normally nest on rocks or boulders, most often utilizing cliff ledges and steep slopes, as well as crevices, gullies, holes or caves. Rocky areas that also prove concealing woodlots as well as, for hunting purposes, that border river valleys and grassy scrubland may be especially attractive.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> If only low rubble is present, they will nest on the ground between rocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Often, in more densely forested areas, they've been recorded nesting on the ground, often among roots of trees, under large bushes and under fallen tree trunks.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Steep slopes with dense vegetation are preferred if nesting on the ground, although some ground nests are surprisingly exposed or in flat spots such as in open spots of the <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a>, ledges of river banks and between wide tree trunks.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> All Eurasian eagle-owl nests in the largely forested <a href="/wiki/Altai_Krai" title="Altai Krai">Altai Krai</a> region of Russia were found to be on the ground, usually at the base of <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">pines</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This species does not often use other bird’s nests as does the great horned owl, which often prefers nests built by other animals over any other nesting site.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl has been recorded in singular cases using nests built by <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>), golden eagle, <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>) and <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black storks</a> (<i>Ciconia nigra</i>). Among the eagle-owls of the fairly heavily wooded wildlands of <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>, they more commonly utilize nests built by other birds than most eagle-owls, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Stork" title="Stork">stork</a> or <a href="/wiki/Accipitrid" class="mw-redirect" title="Accipitrid">accipitrid</a> nests, but a majority of nests are still located on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Gritschik_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gritschik-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> This is contrary to the indication that ground nests are selected only if rocky areas or other bird nests are unavailable, as many will utilize ground nests even where large bird nests seem to be accessible.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gritschik_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gritschik-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Tree holes being used for nesting sites are even more rarely recorded than nests constructed by other birds. While it may be assumed that the eagle-owl is too large to utilize tree hollows, when other large species like the <a href="/wiki/Great_grey_owl" title="Great grey owl">great grey owl</a> have never been recorded nesting in one, the even more robust <a href="/wiki/Blakiston%27s_fish_owl" title="Blakiston&#39;s fish owl">Blakiston's fish owl</a> nests exclusively in cavernous hollows.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Eurasian eagle-owl often uses the same nest site year after year.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG/220px-Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG/330px-Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG/440px-Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1674" data-file-height="1122" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Br%C3%BCtendes_Weibchen.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A brooding female on nest</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Engadin" title="Engadin">Engadin</a>, Switzerland, the male eagle-owl hunts until the young are 4 to 5 weeks old and the female spends all her time brooding at the nest. After this point, the female gradually resumes hunting from both herself and the young and thus provides a greater range of food for the young.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> While it may seem contrary to the species’ highly territorial nature, there is one verified cases of <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamy</a> in Germany, with a male apparently mating with two females, and cooperative brooding in Spain, with a third adult of undetermined sex helping a breeding pair care for the chicks.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The response of Eurasian eagle-owls to humans approaching at the nest is quite variable. The species is often rather less aggressive than some other owls, including related species like the spot-bellied eagle-, great horned and snowy owls, many of the northern <i>Strix</i> species and even some rather smaller owl species, which often fearlessly attack any person found to be nearing their nests. Occasionally, if a person climbs to an active nest, the adult female eagle-owl will do a <a href="/wiki/Distraction_display" title="Distraction display">distraction display</a>, in which they feign an injury. This is an uncommon behaviour in most owls and most often associated with small birds trying to falsely drawl the attention of potential predators away from their offspring.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> More commonly, the female flies off and abandons her nest temporarily, leaving the eggs or small nestlings exposed, when a human approaches it.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, if cornered both adults and nestlings will do an elaborate threat display, also rare in owls in general, in which the eagle-owls raise their wings into a semi-circle and puff up their feathers, followed by a snapping of their bills. Apparently eagle-owls of uncertain and probably exotic origin in Britain are likely to react aggressively to humans approaching the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Also, aggressive encounters involving eagle-owls around their nest, despite being historically rare, apparently have increased in recent decades in Scandinavia.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The discrepancy of aggressiveness at the nest between the Eurasian eagle-owl and its <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">Nearctic counterpart</a> may be correlated to variation in the extent of nest predation that the species endured during the evolutionary process.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs_and_offspring_development">Eggs and offspring development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Eggs and offspring development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG/170px-Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG/255px-Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG/340px-Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2229" data-file-height="2394" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_MWNH_0629.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The eggs are normally laid at intervals of three days and are incubated only by the female. Laying generally begins in late winter but may be later in the year in colder habitats. During the incubation period, the female is brought food at the nest by her mate.<sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A single clutch of white eggs is laid; each egg can measure anything from 56 to 73&#160;mm (2.2 to 2.9&#160;in) long by 44.2 to 53&#160;mm (1.74 to 2.09&#160;in) in width and will usually weigh about 75 to 80&#160;g (2.6 to 2.8&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a>, eggs average 59.8&#160;mm ×&#160;49.5&#160;mm (2.35&#160;in ×&#160;1.95&#160;in), and in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, eggs average 59.4&#160;mm ×&#160;50.1&#160;mm (2.34&#160;in ×&#160;1.97&#160;in). Their eggs are only slightly larger than those of snowy owls and the nominate subspecies of great horned owl, while similar in size to those of <a href="/wiki/Spot-bellied_eagle-owl" title="Spot-bellied eagle-owl">spot-bellied eagle-owls</a> and Blakiston's fish owls. The Eurasian eagle-owl’s eggs are noticeably larger than those of <a href="/wiki/Indian_eagle-owl" title="Indian eagle-owl">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh_eagle-owl" title="Pharaoh eagle-owl">pharaoh eagle-owls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Usually clutch size is 1 to 2, rarely 3 to 4 and exceptionally to 6.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The average number of eggs laid varies with latitude in Europe. Mean clutch size averages 2.02–2.14 in Spain and the <a href="/wiki/Massif" title="Massif">massifs</a> of France, 1.82 to 1.89 in central Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Alps" title="Eastern Alps">eastern Alps</a>; in Sweden and Finland the clutch size averages 1.56 and 1.87, respectively. While variation based on climate is not unusual for different wide-ranging <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">palearctic</a> species, the higher clutch size of western Mediterranean eagle-owls is also probably driven by the presence of <a href="/wiki/Lagomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagomorph">lagomorphs</a> in the diet, which provide high nutritional value than most other regular prey.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The average clutch size, attributed as 2.7, was the lowest of any European owl per one study. One species was attributed with an even lower clutch size in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, the great grey owl with a mean of 2.6, but the mean clutch size was much higher for the same species in Europe, at 4.05.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Spain, incubation is from mid-January to mid-March, hatching and early nestling period is from late March to early April, fledging and post-fledging dependence can be anywhere from mid-April to August and territorial/courtship is anytime hereafter; i.e. the period between the beginning of juvenile dispersal to egg laying; from September to early January.<sup id="cite_ref-Delgado,_M._D._M._2007_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delgado,_M._D._M._2007-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The same general date parameters were followed in southern France.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani3_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In the Italian Alps, the mean egg-laying date was similarly February 27 but the young were more likely to be dependent later, as all fledglings were still being cared for by the end of August and some even lingered under parental care until October.<sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first egg hatches after 31 to 36 days of incubation. The eggs hatch successively; although the average interval between egg-laying is three days, the young tend to hatch no more than a day or two apart.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Like all owls that nest in the open, the downy young are often a mottled grey with some white and buff, which provides camouflage.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> They open their eyes at 4 days of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The chicks grow rapidly, being able to consume small prey whole after roughly three weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-Witherby_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Witherby-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>, it was found that the most noticeable development of the young before they leave the nest was the increase of body size, which was the highest growth rate of any studied owl and faster than either snowy or great horned owls.<sup id="cite_ref-ChickSize_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChickSize-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Body mass increased fourteen times over from 5 days old to 60 days old in this study.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChickSize_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChickSize-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> The male continues to bring prey, leaving in on or around the nest, and the female feeds the nestlings, tearing up the food into suitably-sized pieces. The female resumes hunting after about three weeks which increases the food supply to the chicks. <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">Siblicide</a> has been recorded widely in Eurasian eagle-owls and, according to some authorities, is almost a rule in the species.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Many nesting attempts produce two fledglings, indicating that siblicide is not as common as in other birds of prey, especially some <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> It has been theorized in Spain that males are likely to be the first egg laid to reduce the likelihood of sibling aggression due to the size difference, thus the younger female hatchling is less likely to be killed since it is similar in size to its older sibling.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apparently, the point at which the chicks venture out of the nest is driven by the location of the nest. In elevated nest sites, chicks usually wander out of the nest at 5 to as late as 7 weeks of age, but have been recorded leaving the nest if the nest is on the ground as early as 22 to 25 days old.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The chicks can walk well at five weeks of age and by seven weeks are taking short flights. Hunting and flying skills are not tested prior to the young eagle-owls leaving the nest. Young Eurasian eagle-owls leave the nest by 5–6 weeks of age and typically can be flying weakly (a few metres) by about 7–8 weeks of age. Normally, they are cared for at least another month. By the end of the month, the young eagle-owls are quite assured fliers.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> There are a few confirmed cases of adult eagle-owls in Spain feeding and caring for post-fledgling juvenile eagle-owls that were not their own.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg/220px-Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg/330px-Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg/440px-Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3204" data-file-height="2136" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_bubo_juveniles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Like many large owls, Eurasian eagle-owls leave the nest while still in a functionally flightless state and with large amounts of second down still present, but will fly shortly thereafter.</div></div></div>
<p>A study from southern France found the mean fledgling number of fledgling per nest was 1.67.<sup id="cite_ref-Penteriani3_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penteriani3-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In central Europe, the mean number of fledglings per nest averages between 1.8 and 1.9.<sup id="cite_ref-Radler_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radler-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The mean fledgling rate in the Italian Alps was 1.89, thus being similar. In the Italian Alps it was found that heavier rainfall during breeding decreased fledgling success because it inhibited the ability of the parents to hunt and potentially exposed nestlings to hypothermia.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In the reintroduced population of eagle-owls in <a href="/wiki/Eifel" title="Eifel">Eifel</a>, Germany, occupied territories produced an average of 1.17 fledglings but not all occupying pair attempted to breed, with about 23% of those attempting to breed being unsuccessful.<sup id="cite_ref-Dalbeck_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dalbeck-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In slightly earlier studies, possibly due to higher persecution rates, the mean number of young leaving the nest was often lower, such as 1.77 in <a href="/wiki/Bavaria" title="Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, Germany, 1.1 in <a href="/wiki/Lower_Austria" title="Lower Austria">lower Austria</a> and 0.6 in southern Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> While sibling owls are close in the stage between leaving the nest and fully fledged, about 20 days after leaving the nest, the family unit seems to dissolve and the young disperse quickly and directly.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> All told, the dependence of young eagle-owls on their parents last for 20 to 24 weeks. Independence in central Europe is from September to November. The young leave their parents care normally on their own but are also sometimes chased away by their parents.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The young Eurasian eagle-owls reach sexual maturity by the following year, but do not normally breed until they can establish a territory at around two or three years old.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Until they are able to establish their own territories, young eagle-owls spend their life as nomadic "floaters" and, while they also call, select inconspicuous perch sites unlike breeding birds. Male floaters are especially wary about intrusion into an established territory to avoid potential conspecific aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg/220px-Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg/330px-Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg/440px-Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1117" data-file-height="840" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arkipelet_Solv%C3%A6r%C3%B8yene_har_Europas_tetteste_bestand_av_Hubro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Europe's highest density of Eurasian eagle-owl is reportedly in the <a href="/wiki/Svolv%C3%A6r" title="Svolvær">Svolvær</a> district of <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl has a very wide range across much of Europe and Asia, estimated to be about 32,000,000 square kilometres (12,000,000&#160;sq&#160;mi). In Europe there are estimated to be between 19,000 and 38,000 breeding pairs and in the whole world around 250,000 to 2,500,000 individual birds. The population trend is thought to be decreasing because of human activities, but with such a large range and large total population, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> has rated the bird as being of "<a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Although roughly equal in adaptability and wideness of distribution, the great horned owl, with a total estimated population of up to 5.3 million individuals, apparently has a total population that is roughly twice that of the Eurasian eagle-owl. Numerous factors, including a shorter history of systematic persecution, lesser sensitivity to human disturbance while nesting, somewhat greater ability to adapt to marginal habitats and widespread urbanization and slightly smaller territories, may play into the horned owls greater numbers in modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Longevity">Longevity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Longevity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The eagle-owl can live for up to 20 years in the wild. At one time the oldest ringed eagle-owl was considered a 19-year-old specimen.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Another banded specimen was subsequently found to be 27 years and 9 months old.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Like many other bird species in captivity they can live much longer without having to endure difficult natural conditions, and have possibly survived up to 68 years in zoo collections.<sup id="cite_ref-Mikkola_4-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mikkola-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Healthy adults normally have no natural predators and are thus considered <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a>. The leading causes of death for this species are man-made: electrocution, traffic accidents and shooting frequently claim the life of eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-Konig_16-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Konig-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-owlpages.com_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-owlpages.com-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_mortality">Anthropogenic mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Electrocution was the greatest cause of mortality in 68% of 25 published studies and accounted, on average, for 38.2% of the reported eagle-owl deaths. This was particularly true in the Italian Alps, where the number of dangerous, non-insulated pylons near nests was extremely high, but is highly problematic almost throughout the species’ European distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In one telemetry study, 55% of 27 dispersing young were electrocuted within 1 year of their release from captivity, while electrocution rates of wild-born young are even higher.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Mortality in the Swiss <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine Valley</a> was variable, in radio-tagged, released individuals, most died as a result of starvation (48%) rather than human-based causes but 93% of the wild, un-tagged individuals found dead were due to human activities, 46% due to electrocution and 43% due to collision with vehicles or trains. It was concluded there that insulation of pylons would result in a stabilisation of the local population due to floaters taking up residence in non-occupied territories that formerly held deceased eagle-owls.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Eurasian eagle-owls from Finland were found mainly to die due to electrocution (39%) and collisions with vehicles (22%).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Wind turbine collisions can also be a serious cause of mortality locally.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eagle-owl has been singled out historically as a threat to game species and thus to the economic well-being of landowners, game-keepers and even governmental agencies and as such has been singled out for widespread persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">Local extinctions</a> of Eurasian eagle-owls have been primarily due to persecution. Examples of this including northern Germany in 1830, the Netherlands sometimes in the late nineteenth century, Luxembourg in 1903, Belgium in 1943 and central and western Germany in the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> In trying to determine causes of death for 1476 eagle-owls from Spain, most were unknown and undetermined types of trauma. The largest group that could be determined, 411 birds, was due to collisions, more than half of which were from electrocution, while 313 were due to persecution and merely 85 were directly attributable to natural causes. Clearly, while pylon safety is perhaps the most serious factor to be addressed in Spain, persecution continues to be a massive problem for Spanish eagle-owls. Of seven European nations where modern Eurasian eagle-owl mortality is well-studied, continual persecution is by far the largest problem in Spain, although also continues to be serious (often comprising at least half of studied mortality) in France. From France and Spain, nearly equal numbers of eagle-owls are poisoned (for which raptors might not be the main target), or shot intentionally.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez2_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez2-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_and_re-introductions">Conservation and re-introductions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Conservation and re-introductions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls,_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg/220px-Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg/330px-Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg/440px-Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls%2C_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Baby_Siberian_eagle-owls,_Screech_Owl_Sanctuary.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Siberian eagle-owl chicks in captivity</div></div></div>
<p>While the eagle-owl remains reasonably numerous in some parts of its habitat where nature is still relatively little disturbed by human activity, such as the sparsely populated regions of Russia and <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, concern has been expressed about the future of the Eurasian eagle-owl in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western</a> and central Europe. There, very few areas are not heavily modified by human civilisation, thus exposing the birds to the risk of collisions with deadly man-made objects (e.g. pylons) and a depletion of native prey numbers due to ongoing habitat degradation and urbanisation.<sup id="cite_ref-Hume_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Voous_8-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voous-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Spain, long-term governmental protection of the Eurasian eagle-owl seems to have no positive effect on reducing the persecution of eagle-owls. Therefore, Spanish conservationists have recommended to boost education and stewardship programs in order to protect eagle-owls from direct killing by local residents.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez2_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez2-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Unanimously, biologists studying eagle-owl mortality and conservation factors have recommended to proceed with the proper insulation of electric wires and pylons in areas where the species is present. As this measure is labour-intensive and therefore rather expensive, few efforts have actually been made to insulate pylons in areas with few fiscal resources devoted to conservation such as rural Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-Martinez2_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martinez2-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, a mitigation project was launched in order to insulate transformers that are frequently damaged by eagle-owl electrocution.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Large reintroduction programs were instituted in Germany after the eagle-owl was deemed extinct in the country as a breeding species by the 1960s, as a result of a long period of heavy persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The largest reintroduction there occurred from the 1970s to the 1990s in the Eifel region, near the border with Belgium and Luxembourg. The success of this measure, consisting in more than a thousand eagle-owls being reintroduced at an average cost of $1,500 US dollars per bird, is a subject of controversy. It appears that those eagle-owls reintroduced in the Eifel region which are able to breed successfully, enjoy a nesting success comparable with wild eagle-owls from elsewhere in Europe. On the other hand, mortality levels in the Eifel region appear to remain quite high due to anthropogenic factors. There are also concerns about a lack of genetic diversity of the species in this part of Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-Radler_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radler-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Apparently, the German reintroductions have allowed eagle-owls to repopulate neighbouring parts of Europe, as the breeding populations now occurring in the Low Countries (Netherlands, Belgium and <a href="/wiki/Luxembourg" title="Luxembourg">Luxembourg</a>) are believed to be the result of influx from regions further to the east. Smaller reintroductions have been done elsewhere and the current breeding population in Sweden is believed to be primarily the result of a series of reintroductions.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Conversely to numerous threats and declines incurred by Eurasian eagle-owls, areas where human-dependent non-native prey species such as <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">brown rats</a> (<i>Rattus norvegicus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeons</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>) have flourished, have given the eagle-owls a primary food source and allowed them occupy regions where they were once marginalized or absent.<sup id="cite_ref-Marchesi_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marchesi-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence_in_Great_Britain">Occurrence in Great Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Occurrence in Great Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Eurasian eagle-owl at one time occurred naturally in Great Britain. Some, including the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">RSPB</a>, have claimed that it had disappeared about 10,000–9,000 years ago, after the last <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice age</a>, but fossil remains found in <a href="/wiki/Meare_Lake_Village" title="Meare Lake Village">Meare Lake Village</a> indicate the eagle-owl occurring as recently as roughly 2,000 years ago in the fossil record.<sup id="cite_ref-Warburton_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warburton-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The lack of presence of the Eurasian eagle-owl in British folklore or writings in recent millennium may indicate the lack of occurrence by this species there.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Weald-Artois_Anticline#Formation_of_the_Strait_of_Dover" class="mw-redirect" title="Weald-Artois Anticline">flooding of the land bridge between Britain and continental Europe</a> may have been responsible for their <a href="/wiki/Local_extinction" title="Local extinction">extirpation</a> as they only disperse over limited distances, although early human persecution presumably played a role as well. Some reportages of eagle-owls in Britain have been revealed to actually be <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owls</a> or Indian eagle-owls, the latter a particularly popular owl in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> circuits.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Some breeding pairs do still occur in Britain, though the exact number of pairs and individuals is not definitely known. The World Owl Trust stated that they believe some eagle-owls occurring in <a href="/wiki/North_England" class="mw-redirect" title="North England">North England</a> and Scotland are naturally occurring, making the flight of roughly 350 to 400&#160;km (220 to 250&#160;mi) from the west coast of Norway to <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> and the east coast of Scotland, as well as possibly from the coasts of the Netherlands and Belgium to the south. Although not migratory, eagle-owls can disperse some notable distances in young birds seeking a territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Warburton_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warburton-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior studies of eagle-owl distribution have indicated a strong reluctance to cross large bodies of water in the species. Many authorities state that the Eurasian eagle-owls occurring in Britain are individuals that have escaped from captivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> While, until 19th century, wealthy collectors may have released unwanted eagle-owls, despite press to the contrary, there is no evidence of any organization or individual intentionally releasing eagle-owls recently with the intent to establish a breeding population.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Many feel that the eagle-owl would be classified as an "alien" species. Due to its predatory abilities, many, especially those in the press, have expressed alarm of their effect on "native" species.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> From 1994 to 2007, 73 escaped eagle-owls were not registered as returned, while 50 escapees were re-captured.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Several recorded breeding attempts have been studied and most were unsuccessful, due in large part to incidental disturbance by humans and some due to direct persecution, with eggs having been smashed.<sup id="cite_ref-Melling_72-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Melling-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_conservation-dependent_species">Effect on conservation-dependent species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_eagle-owl&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effect on conservation-dependent species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a highly opportunistic predator, the Eurasian eagle-owl will hunt almost any appropriately sized prey they encounter. Most often they take whatever prey is locally common and can take a large number of species considered harmful to human financial interests, such as <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouse" title="Mouse">mice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Pigeon">pigeons</a>. Eurasian eagle-owls do take rare or endangered species as well. Among the species considered at least vulnerable (up to critically endangered as in the mink and eel, both heavily overexploited by humans) to extinction known to be hunted by Eurasian eagle-owls are <a href="/wiki/Russian_desman" title="Russian desman">Russian desman</a> (<i>Desmana moschata</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Janossy_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyrenean_desman" title="Pyrenean desman">Pyrenean desman</a> (<i>Galemys pyrenaicus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barbastelle" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbastelle">barbastelle</a> (<i>Barbastella barbastellus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_ground_squirrel" title="European ground squirrel">European ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus citellus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_water_vole" title="Southwestern water vole">southwestern water vole</a> (<i>Arvicola sapidus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_mink" title="European mink">European mink</a> (<i>Mustela lutreola</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marbled_polecat" title="Marbled polecat">marbled polecat</a> (<i>Vormela peregusna</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Janossy_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lesser_white-fronted_goose" title="Lesser white-fronted goose">lesser white-fronted goose</a> (<i>Anser erythrops</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Mitev_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vulture</a> (<i>Neophron percnopterus</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_imperial_eagle" title="Eastern imperial eagle">eastern imperial eagle</a> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Houbara_bustard" title="Houbara bustard">houbara bustard</a> (<i>Chlamydotis undulata</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Janossy_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janossy-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Great_bustard" title="Great bustard">great bustard</a> (<i>Otis tarda</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Mitev_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mitev-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spur-thighed_tortoise" class="mw-redirect" title="Spur-thighed tortoise">spur-thighed tortoise</a> (<i>Testudo graeca</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> (<i>Gadus morhua</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/European_eel" title="European eel">European eel</a> (<i>Anguilla anguilla</i>)<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopterus_lumpus" title="Cyclopterus lumpus">lumpfish</a> (<i>Cyclopterus lumpus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Willgohs_115-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willgohs-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
